colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\"><em>C</em>-band low</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">18 14 17.33 \u00b1 0.03</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">34 12 24.9 \u00b1 0.5</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\">\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n            Download table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/suppdata/apj436934t2_ascii.txt?doi=10.1088/0004-637X/755/2/173\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/suppdata/apj436934t2_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>As can be seen in the lower panels of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f3\">3</a>, the positions of component A and of the single detected component in the longer wavelength data are in agreement, within the errors.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj436934fn5\">16</a></sup> With a reduced chi-squared value of 0.984 for the profile fit to a point source in the <em>WISE</em> data, WISE\u00a01814+3412 is unresolved. In W3, with a reduced chi-squared of 0.98, the source FWHM is &lt;1'', while in W4 the reduced chi-squared of 1.09 corresponds to an FWHM &lt; 6''. The separations of 5<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 between component\u00a0A and C, and of 3<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />8 between component A and D, are large compared to the uncertainties in the <em>WISE</em> position, or in the 4.5\u00a0GHz (<em>C</em>-band low) position. We therefore conclude that component A at <em>z</em> = 2.452 is predominantly responsible for the flux from the single component seen in the longer wavelength data, and hereafter refer to component A as WISE\u00a01814+3412 unless otherwise indicated.</p><p>However, Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f3\">3</a> suggests that there is a noticeable shift in the position of component A with respect to component C in the highest resolution images. Component A lies essentially due south of component C in the <em>K<sub>s</sub></em> and <em>Spitzer</em> images, but in the optical images component A is shifted \u22480<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 to the east, or 4\u00a0kpc at <em>z</em> = 2.452. As a point source and <em>z</em> = 2.45 quasar, component C is almost certainly at a fixed position in all images, so we believe this shift in the component A optical centroid position with respect to the <em>K<sub>s</sub></em> and <em>Spitzer</em> positions to be real. Because the optical spectrum and photometry suggest appreciable extinction is present (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-3\">3.3</a>), we consider it most likely that the <em>K<sub>s</sub></em> and <em>Spitzer</em> positions of component A are coincident with the <em>WISE</em> and radio positions, with the optical centroid of component A slightly displaced from these.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj436934s3\" name=\"apj436934s3\">3.\u00a0ANALYSIS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f5\">5</a> compares <em>WISE</em> and <em>Spitzer</em> photometry for WISE\u00a01814+3412 to other unusually red objects redshifted to <em>z</em> = 2.452 and normalized at 22\u2009\u03bcm. These include the well-known ULIRGs Arp 220 and Mrk 231; the extremely red <em>z</em> = 0.058 <em>IRAS</em> source 08572+3915; the <em>z</em> = 1.293 source SST24 J1428+3541 (Desai et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r19\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r19\">2006</a>), which is the brightest 24\u2009\u03bcm source (10.55\u00a0mJy) among mid-IR sources with faint optical counterparts in the 8\u2009deg<sup>2</sup> Bo\u00f6tes field; the <em>z</em> = 2.40 source N2_08 (SWIRE J164216.93+410127.8), which is the most luminous obscured QSO from the sample of Polletta et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r63\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r63\">2008</a>); and source A10 (SWIRE J103916.79+585657.9) from Weedman et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r78\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r78\">2006</a>), which does not have a measured redshift and is among the reddest sources found in the SWIRE survey. None of these are as extreme as WISE\u00a01814+3412 in the W2 to W3 range (4.6\u201312\u2009\u03bcm). Wu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r82\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r82\">2012</a>) show that W1W2-dropouts readily satisfy the <em>R</em> \u2212 [24] &#x2265; 14 criterion for \"dust obscured galaxies\" (DOGs; Dey et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r21\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r21\">2008</a>). Dey et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r21\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r21\">2008</a>) suggest the 24\u2009\u03bcm emission in the brighter DOGs arises from warm dust heated by an AGN, and since the 24\u2009\u03bcm flux density limit for DOGs is ~20 <b>&#x00d7;</b> fainter than for W1W2-dropouts, AGN might be expected to play an important role in W1W2-dropouts.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj436934f5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj436934f5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934f5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934f5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0<em>Spitzer</em> photometry at 3.6 and 4.5\u2009\u03bcm and <em>WISE</em> photometry at 12 and 22\u2009\u03bcm for WISE\u00a01814+3412, compared to SEDs of other unusually red objects whose wavelengths have been scaled to <em>z</em> = 2.452 and flux densities scaled to the 22\u2009\u03bcm flux density of WISE\u00a01814+3412. The approximate flux density scaling factors and observed redshifts are SWIRE J103916.79+585657.9 (4, <em>z</em> unknown but assumed to be 2.452; Weedman et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r78\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r78\">2006</a>); IRAS 08572+3915 (0.06, <em>z</em> = 0.058); Mrk 231 (0.02, <em>z</em> = 0.042); Arp 220 (0.05, <em>z</em> = 0.018); SWIRE N2_08 (SWIRE J164216.93+410127.8, 4, <em>z</em> = 2.40; Polletta et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r63\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r63\">2008</a>); and SST24 J1428+3541 (2.3, <em>z</em> = 1.293; Desai et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r19\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r19\">2006</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj436934f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/downloadFigure/figure/apj436934f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj436934f5\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj436934f5\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><h3 id=\"apj436934s3-1\" name=\"apj436934s3-1\">3.1.\u00a0SED Modeling</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f6\">6</a> plots the observed photometry for WISE\u00a01814+3412. The detections in W3 (12\u2009\u03bcm), W4 (22\u2009\u03bcm), and 350\u2009\u03bcm and the upper limits at 450\u2009\u03bcm and 1.1\u00a0mm imply the luminosity (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-2\">3.2</a>) is dominated by emission from warm dust, possibly heated by an AGN. The EVLA detections show an AGN is present (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-6\">3.6</a>). The similarity of the WISE\u00a01814+3412 optical spectrum to an LBG (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s2-2\">2.2</a>) implies that active star formation is underway, as discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-3\">3.3</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj436934f6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj436934f6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934f6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934f6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0Best-fit SED template model (heavy gray line) to the photometry (shown by green symbols) for WISE\u00a01814+3412 plotted in \u03bd<em>F</em><sub>\u03bd</sub> units. Other measurements (black symbols) and upper limits (black downward pointing arrows) were not used for the template fit. The template fit uses starburst (blue) and Sbc spiral (green) components from Assef et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r2\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r2\">2010</a>), and the Richards et al. (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r65\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r65\">2006a</a>) Type 1 AGN template (red) with <em>A<sub>V</sub></em> = 48 extinction applied. The cyan line shows a 488\u00a0K blackbody fit to the W3 (12\u2009\u03bcm) and W4 (22\u2009\u03bcm) photometry which overlaps the red curve below 12\u2009\u03bcm, while the magenta line shows a modified (i.e., with dust emissivity \u221d\u03bd<sup>1.5</sup>) 45\u00a0K blackbody fit to the 350\u2009\u03bcm flux density and the upper limit at 1.1\u00a0mm. The dotted line shows an <em>F</em><sub>\u03bd</sub>\u221d\u03bd<sup>\u22120.8</sup> power-law fit to the radio data. The gray dashed line shows the power laws used to estimate the minimum bolometric luminosity in Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-2\">3.2</a>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj436934f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/downloadFigure/figure/apj436934f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj436934f6\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj436934f6\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Motivated by these components, in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f6\">6</a> we show an example fit to the optical, near-IR, <em>Spitzer</em>,\u00a0 and <em>WISE</em> photometry for WISE\u00a01814+3412\u00a0 using starburst and evolved stellar population templates from Assef et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r2\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r2\">2010</a>), and a reddened Type\u00a01 AGN template from Richards et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r65\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r65\">2006a</a>).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj436934fn6\">17</a></sup> As in Assef et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r2\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r2\">2010</a>), the reddening is assumed to follow a Small-Magellanic-Cloud-like extinction curve (Gordon &amp; Clayton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r29\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r29\">1998</a>) for \u03bb &lt; 3300\u2009\u00c5 and a Galactic extinction curve (Cardelli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r13\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r13\">1989</a>) at longer wavelengths, in both cases with <em>R<sub>V</sub></em> = 3.1. The resultant AGN template extinctions in the rest frame are <em>E</em>(<em>B</em> \u2212 <em>V</em>) = 15.6 \u00b1 1.4 or <em>A<sub>V</sub></em> = 48 \u00b1 4, corresponding to <em>A</em><sub>W3</sub> = 2.7 \u00b1 0.2 and <em>A</em><sub>W4</sub> = 0.98 \u00b1 0.09 in the observed frame. Because the Richards et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r65\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r65\">2006a</a>) template does not extend to 350\u2009\u03bcm (100\u2009\u03bcm rest), this reddened Type 1 AGN component was <em>not</em> fit to the submillimeter photometry, but with a small extrapolation it nevertheless provides a good match to those data. The cyan line in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f6\">6</a> shows a 490\u00a0K blackbody fit to the W3 (12\u2009\u03bcm) and W4 (22\u2009\u03bcm) photometry, while the magenta line shows a modified (i.e., with dust emissivity \u221d\u03bd<sup>1.5</sup>) 45\u00a0K blackbody fit to the 350\u2009\u03bcm flux density and the upper limit at 1.1\u00a0mm. Finally, the dotted line shows a radio spectral index \u03b1 = 0.8 fit to the EVLA data (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s2-1-6\">2.1.6</a>). We consider each of these components in more detail below.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj436934s3-2\" name=\"apj436934s3-2\">3.2.\u00a0Luminosity Estimates</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We obtain a lower limit of <em>L</em><sub>bol</sub> &gt; 3.7 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub> for the bolometric luminosity of WISE\u00a01814+3412 by assuming the spectral energy distribution (SED) is given by two power laws connecting the observed flux densities at W3, W4, and 350\u2009\u03bcm, with no luminosity at shorter or longer wavelengths, as shown by the light dashed lines in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f6\">6</a>. The luminosity can also be estimated by integrating over the best-fit SED templates of Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-1\">3.1</a>, which are dominated by the dust-reddened AGN component, extending the latter beyond 350\u2009\u03bcm via an <em>F</em><sub>\u03bd</sub>\u221d\u03bd<sup>3.5</sup> power law. The details of this extrapolation are not significant as nearly all of the flux is already contained in the reddened AGN template. This approach yields a bolometric luminosity estimate of 9.2 \u00b1 1.0 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub>, of which 7.2 \u00b1 0.8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>11</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (&lt;1%) is from the stellar components. From the upper limit to the <em>IRAS</em> 60\u2009\u03bcm flux density (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s2-1-4\">2.1.4</a>) plotted in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f6\">6</a>, the luminosity is unlikely to be a factor of two higher.</p><h4 id=\"apj436934s3-2-1\" name=\"apj436934s3-2-1\">3.2.1.\u00a0Lensing</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Luminosities approaching 10<sup>14</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub> inevitably lead to suspicions that WISE\u00a01814+3412 is gravitationally lensed, as is true for sources such as IRAS FSC10214+4724 (e.g., Eisenhardt et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r23\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r23\">1996</a>) and APM 08279+5255 (e.g., Ibata et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r40\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r40\">1999</a>). However, Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f3\">3</a> and\u00a0<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f4\">4</a> show lensing is unlikely to be a significant factor for WISE\u00a01814+3412. Although there are multiple components at the same redshift (A, C, D), their SEDs and spectra have different characteristics. The obvious candidate for a foreground lens is component B, but this appears to be a Galactic M dwarf star (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s2-2\">2.2</a>) rather than a massive galaxy at intermediate redshift. The extended <em>K<sub>s</sub></em> emission at P.A. ~ 140\u00b0 around component A seen in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f3\">3</a> is unlikely to come from a foreground lensing galaxy, as it coincides with extended <em>z</em> = 2.452 Ly\u03b1 emission at the same P.A. (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s2-2\">2.2</a>). Therefore, the large luminosity of WISE\u00a01814+3412 appears to be intrinsic.</p></div></div><h3 id=\"apj436934s3-3\" name=\"apj436934s3-3\">3.3.\u00a0Starburst Component</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The spectrum of WISE\u00a01814+3412 is typical of an LBG (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s2-2\">2.2</a>), implying active star formation directly detected in the rest-frame UV. Assuming no UV extinction, the SFR from the average <em>L</em><sub>\u03bd</sub> of the starburst component of our fit (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-1\">3.1</a>) over rest 1500\u20132800\u2009\u00c5 and Equation (1) of Kennicutt (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r44\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r44\">1998</a>) is ~50\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup> for a Salpeter initial mass function (IMF) from 0.1 to 100\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>, or ~30\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup> for a Chabrier (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r14\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r14\">2003</a>) IMF.</p><p>However, even UV-selected LBG galaxies show evidence for extinction (Shapley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r74\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r74\">2003</a>). We estimate the extinction in WISE\u00a01814+3412 by comparing the observed <em>g</em>' \u2212 <em>r</em>' = 0.34 color (AB system) to the color expected from Bruzual &amp; Charlot (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r11\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r11\">2003</a>) models, using the \"Ez_Gal\" Web tool.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj436934fn7\">18</a></sup> Continuously star-forming models with Salpeter and Chabrier IMFs, solar and 0.4 solar metallicities, and ages of 0.1\u20130.4\u00a0Gyr were considered. The model <em>g</em>' \u2212 <em>r</em>' colors range from 0.01 for solar metallicity models which have been forming stars for 0.4\u00a0Gyr to \u22120.12 for 0.1\u00a0Gyr models with 0.4 solar metallicity, and do not change substantially between the two IMFs. Using the reddening law from Equation (4) of Calzetti et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r12\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r12\">2000</a>), these colors imply extinction at 0.16\u2009\u03bcm ranging from 2.00 to 2.75\u00a0mag. The corresponding corrected SFR values are ~300\u2009(180) to 600\u2009(350)\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup> for a Salpeter (Chabrier) IMF. Higher SFR values are possible if some is completely obscured in the UV.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj436934s3-4\" name=\"apj436934s3-4\">3.4.\u00a0Stellar Component</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The stellar mass can be estimated from the observed 17.13\u00a0mag at 4.5\u2009\u03bcm, the longest wavelength in the SED dominated by the stellar component (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f6\">6</a>). The starburst component of the template fit (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-1\">3.1</a>) falls well below the observed <em>K<sub>s</sub></em>, [3.6], and [4.5] SED, requiring a significant contribution from older stars, as represented by the Sbc template.</p><p>An upper limit for the plausible stellar mass can be obtained using the Bruzual &amp; Charlot (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r11\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r11\">2003</a>) models by assuming that the observed [4.5] flux density is from stars formed in a 0.1\u00a0Gyr burst at <em>z</em> = 10 (i.e., 2\u00a0Gyr before they are observed at <em>z</em> = 2.452) with a Salpeter IMF and solar metallicity. This model predicts <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> = 0.51 in rest <em>K</em>, much higher than the ~0.1 value typical of the starburst models considered in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-3\">3.3</a>, but still well below the value of ~1 typical of present-day galaxies (Bell et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r4\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r4\">2003</a>). Allowing for extinction of 0.34\u00a0mag at [4.5] corresponding to the upper end of the range inferred in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-3\">3.3</a> from the observed versus model <em>g</em>' \u2212 <em>r</em>' colors, this approach gives an upper limit to the stellar mass of 5.8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>11</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. While larger extinction values and hence masses are of course possible, this value is already many times the stellar mass of a field <em>L</em>* galaxy today (~5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>10</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>; e.g., Baldry et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r3\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r3\">2008</a>).</p><p>A lower limit can be obtained assuming that all the observed [4.5] light comes from one of the starburst models considered in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-3\">3.3</a>. The smallest stellar mass obtained with this approach is 4.1 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>10</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>, for a 0.1\u00a0Gyr old burst with solar metallicity and a Chabrier IMF. A more plausible model with equal contributions to the [4.5] luminosity from a starburst and an evolved stellar population, and with a more typical extinction at [4.5] of 0.3\u00a0mag, yields a stellar mass of 3.6\u2009(2.0) <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>11</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>, for a Salpeter/Chabrier IMF.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj436934s3-5\" name=\"apj436934s3-5\">3.5.\u00a0Dust Component</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The prominent emission of WISE\u00a01814+3412 in W3 and W4 implies a significant contribution from warm dust. Fitting a blackbody to the W3 to W4 flux density ratio of 0.13 at <em>z</em> = 2.452 yields a temperature of ~490\u00a0K (cyan curve in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f6\">6</a>). Of course a range of temperatures is needed to account for all the data, but a modified blackbody (with <em>F</em><sub>\u03bd</sub>\u221d\u03bd<sup>1.5</sup><em>B</em><sub>\u03bd</sub>) fit to the W4 to 350\u2009\u03bcm ratio of 0.44 also implies warm dust (~170 K). The scale of the warm dust is substantial: 30\u2013250\u00a0pc for the radius of a spherical blackbody with <em>L</em><sub>bol</sub> = 9 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub> and temperatures of 490\u2013170\u00a0K. Using Equation (1) of Calzetti et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r12\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r12\">2000</a>) with <em>F</em><sub>350</sub>/(1 + <em>z</em>) as the rest 100\u2009\u03bcm flux density, the warm dust mass is <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10<sup>7</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. The gas mass is <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> for the Milky Way gas-to-dust ratio, but could be up to ~10<sup>11</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> using the gas-to-dust ratio in AGNs (Maiolino et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r54\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r54\">2001</a>).</p><p>In contrast, the ratio of the 350\u2009\u03bcm to 1.1\u00a0mm flux density is greater than 13.75, which means that dust at the cooler temperatures associated with submillimeter galaxies (~35\u00a0K; e.g., Kov\u00e1cs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r48\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r48\">2006</a>) plays a smaller role in the bolometric luminosity of this source. Wu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r82\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r82\">2012</a>) find that this predominance of warm dust is a characteristic of W1W2-dropouts, using CSO observations of 26 sources. For WISE\u00a01814+3412, dust cooler than 45\u00a0K with <em>F</em><sub>\u03bd</sub>\u221d\u03bd<sup>1.5</sup><em>B</em><sub>\u03bd</sub> normalized to the observed 350\u2009\u03bcm flux density would exceed the 2.4\u00a0mJy 95% confidence upper limit at 1.1\u00a0mm (magenta curve in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f6\">6</a>).</p><p>Nevertheless, it seems likely that WISE\u00a01814+3412 contains a cooler dust component. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f3\">3</a> (see also Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s2-3\">2.3</a>) shows the rest-UV emission from component A is displaced from the rest optical and near-IR by ~4 kpc, suggesting extinction on a much larger scale than the warm dust. The <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub> expected from a ~300\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup> SFR (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-3\">3.3</a>) is ~2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>12</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (Kennicutt <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r44\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r44\">1998</a>). Dust with this luminosity at 35\u00a0K with <em>F</em><sub>\u03bd</sub>\u221d\u03bd<sup>1.5</sup><em>B</em><sub>\u03bd</sub> would contribute more than half the 350\u2009\u03bcm flux density and just satisfy the 1.1\u00a0mm limit, and have a mass of ~2.5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>8</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj436934s3-6\" name=\"apj436934s3-6\">3.6.\u00a0Radio Source</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The rest 1.4\u00a0GHz luminosity is ~5 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>25</sup>\u2009W\u2009Hz<sup>\u22121</sup>, and the ratio of rest 5\u00a0GHz (~observed 1.4\u00a0GHz) to rest 4400\u2009\u00c5 (~observed <em>J</em>) flux densities is ~200. Both metrics qualify WISE\u00a01814+3412 as radio loud (Stern et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r76\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r76\">2000</a>). If free\u2013free emission from H\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> regions associated with star formation were present in WISE\u00a01814+3412 at the level seen in M82 (Condon <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r16\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r16\">1992</a>, Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f1\">1</a>), the Ka band upper limit of 175\u2009\u03bcJy limit (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s2-1-6\">2.1.6</a>) should have been exceeded, but the limit is consistent with the 115\u2009\u03bcJy flux density expected from extrapolating the EVLA observations. This argues against star formation dominating the energetics of WISE\u00a01814+3412.</p><p>Using the template SED fit and radio properties to estimate the <em>z</em> = 2.452 rest-frame flux densities at 60\u2009\u03bcm, 100\u2009\u03bcm, and 1.4\u00a0GHz (90, 33\u00a0mJy, and 3.9\u00a0mJy, respectively), we obtain <em>q</em> = 1.36 from Equations (14) and (15) of Condon (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r16\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r16\">1992</a>). This is ~10 <b>&#x00d7;</b> more radio loud than the normal value of 2.4 \u00b1 0.24 (Ivison et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r41\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r41\">2010</a>), again implying the presence of an AGN. We conclude the radio emission in WISE\u00a01814+3412 is due to AGN activity, rather than to star formation.</p></div><h3 id=\"apj436934s3-7\" name=\"apj436934s3-7\">3.7.\u00a0AGN Component</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Over 99% of the bolometric luminosity in the template fit to the SED comes from the reddened Type 1 AGN component (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-2\">3.2</a>), although there are no AGN features in the component A spectrum (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s2-2\">2.2</a>). Wu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r82\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r82\">2012</a>) show W1W2-dropouts exhibit a range of rest-UV spectra, from an absence of any AGN signatures to the presence of broad lines, with most showing AGN features. The most direct evidence for an AGN in WISE\u00a01814+3412 comes from the radio emission, which is above the radio\u2013IR correlation (Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-6\">3.6</a>). Since the SED modeling finds <em>A<sub>V</sub></em> = 48\u00a0mag, corresponding to ~200\u00a0mag extinction in the rest UV, it is not surprising that AGN features such as broad lines and UV continuum from close to the black hole are absent from the spectrum. The corresponding proton column to the AGN is ~10<sup>23</sup>\u2009cm<sup>\u22122</sup> for Milky Way reddening (Bohlin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r8\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r8\">1978</a>), but up to ~10<sup>25</sup>\u2009cm<sup>\u22122</sup> (i.e., Compton-thick) using gas-to-dust ratios for AGNs (Maiolino et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r54\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r54\">2001</a>). If the source hosts an extremely luminous and obscured AGN, data from approved <em>XMM-Newton</em> and <em>NuSTAR</em> (Harrison et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r32\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r32\">2010</a>) programs should reveal a hard X-ray spectrum, with few photons below rest-frame 10\u00a0keV.</p><p>Deep spectropolarimetry might detect AGN features. Such work has been done for example for <em>z</em> ~ 2 radio galaxies with similar optical brightness, with exposures typically needing most of a Keck night per target (Dey et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r20\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r20\">1996</a>; Cimatti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r15\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r15\">1998</a>), and IRAS FSC10214+4724 (Goodrich et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r28\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r28\">1996</a>; optically much brighter than WISE\u00a01814+3412). All these objects show high ionization, narrow emission lines in their unpolarized spectra, and the lack of such features in WISE\u00a01814+3412 is interesting, since typically they come from regions on a kpc scale (e.g., Bennert et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r5\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r5\">2002</a>). This suggests that either the narrow line region is more compact for this source and/or that obscuring material is present on larger scales than the torus (e.g., Brand et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r9\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r9\">2007</a>). The shift between the rest-UV and optical continuum (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f3\">3</a>) provides support for this idea.</p><p>If the luminosity in WISE\u00a01814+3412 is due primarily to accretion onto an SMBH, the SMBH mass for the template fit AGN luminosity is 2.8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>9</sup>(<em>L</em><sub>Edd</sub>/<em>L</em><sub>bol</sub>)\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>, where (<em>L</em><sub>bol</sub>/<em>L</em><sub>Edd</sub>) is the Eddington ratio. The dust sublimation radius is ~7 pc, and the outer radius of the dust torus is expected to be no more than 30 times the sublimation radius (Nenkova et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r59\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r59\">2008</a>). This size is consistent with the range discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apj436934s3-5\">3.5</a>, but larger scale dust would be needed to obscure a kpc scale narrow line region.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj436934s4\" name=\"apj436934s4\">4.\u00a0SUMMARY AND DISCUSSION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>With a minimum bolometric luminosity of 3.7 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub>, and more likely <em>L</em><sub>bol</sub> ~ 9 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub>, WISE\u00a01814+3412 easily qualifies as a hyperluminous infrared galaxy, the first identified by <em>WISE</em>. Its W4 (22\u2009\u03bcm) flux density and redshift exceed that of the brightest 24\u2009\u03bcm source DOG in the 8\u2009deg<sup>2</sup> Bo\u00f6tes field (SST24 J1428+3541; Desai et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r19\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r19\">2006</a>), giving it a rest 5\u2009\u03bcm luminosity three times higher. Its luminosity probably exceeds the estimated 4 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>13</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub> of the most luminous obscured quasar in Polletta et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r63\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r63\">2008</a>; SWIRE J164216.93+410127.8), which has four times lower flux at 24\u2009\u03bcm. It approaches the ~2 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>14</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub> of the most luminous known quasars such as S5 0014+813 (Kuhr et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r49\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r49\">1983</a>), HS 1700+6416 (Reimers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r64\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r64\">1989</a>), and SDSS J074521.78+473436.2 (Schneider et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r73\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r73\">2005</a>). Several W1W2-dropouts reported in Wu et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r82\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r82\">2012</a>) are even more luminous than WISE\u00a01814+3412, with <em>minimum</em> <em>L</em><sub>bol</sub> up to 1.8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>14</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub>.</p><p>WISE\u00a01814+3412 is also a massive, actively star-forming LBG, with an extinction-corrected UV SFR of ~300\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup> and a stellar mass of ~3 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>11</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (several times the stellar mass of a field <em>L</em>* galaxy today; Baldry et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r3\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r3\">2008</a>). The specific SFR of ~1\u2009Gyr<sup>\u22121</sup> places WISE\u00a01814+3412 in the starburst galaxy regime, but even the upper end of the extinction corrected UV SFR estimates account for <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10% of the bolometric luminosity. With no AGN signatures in the UV spectrum, it is tempting to assume that additional dust-obscured star formation accounts for the high bolometric luminosity. However, the upper limit at 1.1\u00a0mm would be greatly exceeded unless the star-forming dust was significantly warmer than 35\u00a0K.</p><p>This possibility merits consideration. Kov\u00e1cs et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r48\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r48\">2006</a>) present evidence that starburst dominated submillimeter galaxies have higher temperatures at higher redshifts and luminosities. The SED of the extreme low-metallicity dwarf starburst galaxy SBS 0335-052 peaks at 30\u2009\u03bcm (Houck et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r39\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r39\">2004</a>), and Hirashita &amp; Hunt (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r35\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r35\">2004</a>) suggest that virtually all high-redshift star formation may occur in the \"active mode\" for which SBS 0335-052 is the prototype. The active mode is characterized by dense, compact star-forming regions in which dust from core-collapse supernovae warms as it screens the gas from UV photons, enabling runaway star formation as the gas continues to cool on a dynamical timescale. Yet it is not obvious that this mechanism can be scaled up to the levels required to produce ~10<sup>14</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub>, i.e., an SFR ~10<sup>4</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>\u2009yr<sup>\u22121</sup> and ~10<sup>8</sup> O stars.</p><p>Since WISE\u00a01814+3412 contains a modestly powerful radio source, an AGN is almost certainly present. An obscured AGN powered by an SMBH could supply the luminosity while being consistent with the absence of AGN signatures in the UV spectrum. In fact, despite the clear starburst signature from the optical spectrum, the lack of submillimeter emission and predominance of hot dust suggest that in WISE\u00a01814+3412 (and other W1W2-dropouts; Wu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r82\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r82\">2012</a>) we may be seeing an SED more like the \"intrinsic\" SED from the dusty central AGN region than is the case for many quasars (see Figure 6 of Netzer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r60\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r60\">2007</a>).</p><p>The formation and coevolution of AGN and stellar populations is the subject of much current research. Theoretical scenarios suggest the initial starburst rapidly enshrouds a forming galaxy in cold dust which emits at submillimeter wavelengths. This is followed by an increase in fueling of the SMBH, triggering an AGN, generating warmer dust and mid-IR emission. Finally, outflows from the AGN or starburst clear the dust and gas, revealing an optical quasar and then removing the fuel, which powers both starburst and quasar, leaving a quiescent massive galaxy (e.g., Sanders et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r69\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r69\">1988</a>; Hopkins et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r38\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r38\">2006</a>; Narayanan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r58\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r58\">2009</a>).</p><p>The black hole mass needed to power the bolometric luminosity is 2.8 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>9</sup>(<em>L</em><sub>Edd</sub>/<em>L</em><sub>bol</sub>)\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub>. But using the local <em>L</em><sub>bulge</sub>/<em>M</em><sub>BH</sub> relation, the SED fit stellar component absolute magnitude of <em>M<sub>K</sub></em> = \u221226.28 gives a black hole mass of only 1.4 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>9</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> (Graham <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r30\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r30\">2007</a>). If the relation is actually between SMBH mass and bulge <em>mass</em> rather than bulge luminosity, as seems plausible, the discrepancy is larger. At <em>z</em> = 2.452 the stellar mass for a given <em>K</em> luminosity is likely less than half as large as today. Therefore, assuming the bolometric luminosity of the AGN template fit is correct, and that the Graham (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r30\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r30\">2007</a>) relation adjusted for lower (<em>M</em>/<em>L</em>)<sub><em>K</em></sub> at <em>z</em> = 2.45 applies, the implied Eddington ratio is <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 4. For comparison, Kollmeier et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r46\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r46\">2006</a>) measure a typical Eddington ratio in luminous quasars of 0.25, and Kelly et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r43\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r43\">2010</a>) find 0.1 to be representative. If WISE\u00a01814+3412 has a similar Eddington ratio, then one of these assumptions does not hold.</p><p>The local stellar mass to SMBH mass relation could apply if the bolometric luminosity is mainly from star formation with warm dust similar to that in low-metallicity starburst dwarf galaxies, but scaled up a factor of a thousand. There is evidence that starbursts can dominate luminosities even in sources with spectral signatures of quasars (e.g., Netzer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r60\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r60\">2007</a>; Polletta et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r63\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r63\">2008</a>). Alternatively, the stellar mass may have been underestimated by a factor of several due to greater extinction at 4.5\u2009\u03bcm, but the extinction corrected stellar mass of &gt;10<sup>12</sup>\u2009<em>M</em><sub>\u2609</sub> is then extreme. Note this argument holds for AGN with <em>L</em> &gt; 10<sup>14</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub> in general, and the largest SMBH masses in the most luminous objects may be well above the normal relation (e.g., McConnell et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r57\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r57\">2012</a>; Salviander &amp; Shields <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r67\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r67\">2012</a>). Another possibility is that WISE\u00a01814+3412 is in an unstable transitional stage where the Eddington ratio is well above 1. Recently, Kawakatu &amp; Ohsuga (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r42\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r42\">2011</a>) have argued that SMBHs with super-Eddington accretion will be characterized by a low ratio of near-IR to bolometric luminosity, which is essentially the selection criterion for WISE\u00a01814+3412.</p><p>We consider it more likely that WISE\u00a01814+3412 lies well off the local <em>M</em><sub>BH</sub>\u2013bulge mass relation, suggesting it may be in an early stage of its evolution where AGN activity is dominant and the object has yet to build the bulk of its stellar mass. This would, however, contradict the expectation that AGN activity should lag the peak of star formation (see, e.g., Hopkins <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r37\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r37\">2012</a>). In this context, the presence of three systems (components A, C, and D) at the same redshift and within 50\u00a0kpc suggests that a significant amount of stellar mass assembly lies in WISE\u00a01814+3412's future.</p></div><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The authors thank Alex Pope for insightful discussions regarding the dust content of WISE\u00a01814+3412; Alice Shapley for assistance in determining the star formation rate associated with a Lyman break galaxy; R. S. McMillan, J. V. Scotti, J. A. Larsen, and G. J. Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer, Spitzer (IRAC) (MIPS) - Spitzer Space Telescope satellite, Hale (WIRC) (LFC) - Palomar Observatory's 5.1m Hale Telescope, Keck:I (LRIS) - KECK I Telescope, Keck:II (NIRC2) - KECK II Telescope, Mayall (Mosaic) - Kitt Peak National Observatory's 4 meter Mayall Telescope, CSO (SHARC II) (Bolocam) - Caltech Submillimeter Observatory, EVLA - Expanded Very Large Array, GBT - Green Bank Telescope</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 id=\"footnotes\">Footnotes</h2>\n\t\t<div data-mobile-collapse=\"\">\n\t\t\t<ul class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>The <em>WISE</em> catalog coordinates listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj436934t2\">2</a> are in agreement with the coordinates for component A in other bands, and have a systematic uncertainty with respect to 2MASS of &lt;0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2. The world coordinate system (WCS) in <em>WISE</em> image tiles deviates slightly but systematically from the catalog coordinates for sources in the tile, with the catalog coordinates being correct. For the image tile containing WISE\u00a01814+3412 this offset causes sources to appear 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />68 east and 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />10 south of their overlaid catalog coordinates, and we have adjusted the <em>WISE</em> image WCS to correct for this in the bottom center panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f3\">3</a>.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apj436934fn6\" id=\"apj436934fn6\">17\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p>Due to its longer wavelength range, the Richards et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r65\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r65\">2006a</a>) AGN template was used rather than that from Assef et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r2\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r2\">2010</a>), although the fit is qualitatively identical with either template.</p></p></div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"apj436934fn7\" id=\"apj436934fn7\">18\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\"><p class=\"mb-0\"><p><a <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">THE FIRST HYPER-LUMINOUS INFRARED GALAXY DISCOVERED BY <I>WISE</I>       </h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Peter R. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F755%2F2%2F173\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/755/2/173</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n                      <P>We report the discovery by the <I>Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</I> (<I>WISE</I>) of the <I>z</I> = 2.452 source WISE\u00a0J181417.29+341224.9, the first hyperluminous source found in the <I>WISE</I> survey. WISE\u00a01814+3412 is also the prototype for an all-sky sample of ~1000 extremely luminous \"W1W2-dropouts\" (sources faint or undetected by <I>WISE</I> at 3.4 and 4.6 \u03bcm and well detected at 12 or 22 \u03bcm). The <I>WISE</I> data and a 350 \u03bcm detection give a minimum bolometric luminosity of 3.7 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<SUP>13</SUP> <I>L</I>             <SUB>\u2609</SUB>, with ~10<SUP>14</SUP> <I>L</I>             <SUB>\u2609</SUB> plausible. Follow-up images reveal four nearby sources: a QSO and two Lyman break galaxies (LBGs) at <I>z</I> = 2.45, and an M dwarf star. The brighter LBG dominates the bolometric emission. Gravitational lensing is unlikely given the source locations and their different spectra and colors. The dominant LBG spectrum indicates a star formation rate ~300 <I>M</I>             <SUB>\u2609</SUB> yr<SUP>\u20131</SUP>, accounting for <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 10% of the bolometric luminosity. Strong 22 \u03bcm emission relative to 350 \u03bcm implies that warm dust contributes significantly to the luminosity, while cooler dust normally associated with starbursts is constrained by an upper limit at 1.1\u00a0mm. Radio emission is ~10 times above the far-infrared/radio correlation, indicating an active galactic nucleus (AGN) is present. An obscured AGN combined with starburst and evolved stellar components can account for the observations. If the black hole mass follows the local <I>M</I>             <SUB>BH</SUB>-bulge mass relation, the implied Eddington ratio is <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 4. WISE\u00a01814+3412 may be a heavily obscured object where the peak AGN activity occurred prior to the peak era of star formation.</P>       </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/755/2/173&doi=10.1088/0004-637X/755/2/173&exportFormat=iopexport_bib&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-bib\" title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/755/2/173&doi=10.1088/0004-637X/755/2/173&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2012ApJ...755..173E\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2012ApJ...755..173E&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2012ApJ...755..173E\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj436934s1\" name=\"apj436934s1\">1.\u00a0INTRODUCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The <em>Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</em> (<em>WISE</em>) launched on 2009 December 14, and began surveying the sky on 2010 January 14, completing its first full coverage in 2010 July. <em>WISE</em> achieves 5\u03c3 point-source sensitivities of better than 0.08, 0.11, 1, and 6\u00a0mJy at 3.4, 4.6, 12, and 22\u2009\u03bcm (hereafter referred to as W1, W2, W3, and W4) in a single coverage on the ecliptic, consisting of eight or more exposures at each sky location (Wright et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r81\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r81\">2010</a>). Sensitivity improves away from the ecliptic due to denser exposure overlap and lower zodiacal background. The survey continued in W1 and W2 after the cryogen was exhausted at the end of 2010 September, and concluded on 2011 February 1, having achieved two complete sky coverages in these two bands. The <em>WISE</em> all-sky data release was issued on 2012 March 14.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apj436934fn1\">12</a></sup></p><p>The primary science objectives for <em>WISE</em> are to identify the coldest and nearest brown dwarfs to the Sun (see, e.g., Mainzer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r53\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r53\">2011b</a>; Cushing et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r18\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r18\">2011</a>; Kirkpatrick et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r45\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r45\">2011</a>), and the most luminous, dusty, forming galaxies (ultraluminous infrared galaxies or ULIRGs). With regard to the most luminous objects, the dominant sources of energy production in the universe are fusion in stars and gravitational accretion onto supermassive black holes (SMBHs). The tight correlation between the masses of SMBHs at the centers of galaxies and the masses of the stellar bulges in these galaxies (Magorrian et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r51\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r51\">1998</a>; Ferrarese &amp; Merritt <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r25\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r25\">2000</a>; Gebhardt et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r27\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r27\">2000</a>) implies the two formation processes are intimately connected. Bulge stellar populations are old and quiescent today, and so must have formed in the distant past, when the cosmic star formation rate (SFR) was much higher (e.g., Hopkins <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r36\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r36\">2004</a>). Similarly the peak era of accretion onto SMBHs was at redshifts <em>z</em> ~ 2, when luminous quasars were common (e.g., Richards et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r66\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r66\">2006b</a>; Assef et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r1\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r1\">2011</a>). There is substantial evidence that dust absorbs much of the UV/optical luminosity generated by the formation of massive galaxies and their central black holes at <em>z</em> &gt; 1 (e.g., Blain et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r6\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r6\">2004</a>; Le Floc'h et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r49a\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r49a\">2005</a>; Stern et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r77\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r77\">2005</a>; Hickox et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r33\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r33\">2007</a>). The dust is heated in the process, and most of the luminosity emerges at IR wavelengths, creating a ULIRG.</p><p>The most extreme examples of ULIRGs are therefore likely associated with the major formation events of the most massive galaxies. Such objects should appear in the sensitive infrared all-sky <em>WISE</em> survey, and may be missed in surveys with substantially smaller areas by, e.g., <em>Spitzer</em> and <em>Herschel</em>. Here we report on WISE\u00a0J181417.29+341224.9 (hereafter WISE\u00a01814+3412), the first hyperluminous infrared galaxy (<em>L</em><sub>IR</sub> &gt; 10<sup>13</sup>\u2009<em>L</em><sub>\u2609</sub>) discovered by <em>WISE</em>.</p><p>Magnitudes are converted to flux densities using zero-point values of 3631\u00a0Jy for the AB system <em>g</em>' and <em>r</em>' bands. Other magnitudes are on the Vega system, using zero-points of 1594 and 666.7\u00a0Jy for the Two Micron All Sky Survey (2MASS) system <em>J</em> and <em>K<sub>s</sub></em> bands; 280.9 and 179.7\u00a0Jy for <em>Spitzer</em> IRAC [3.6] and [4.5]; and 306.7, 170.7, 29.04, and 8.284\u00a0Jy for <em>WISE</em> W1 through W4, respectively (Wright et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r81\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r81\">2010</a>). Luminosities are calculated assuming \u03a9<sub><em>M</em></sub> = 0.3, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934ieqn1.gif\" alt=\"$\\Omega _\\Lambda = 0.7$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\Omega _\\Lambda = 0.7</script></span></span>, and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> = 70\u2009km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apj436934s2\" name=\"apj436934s2\">2.\u00a0SELECTION CRITERIA AND FOLLOW-UP OBSERVATIONS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Early searches for the most luminous galaxies with <em>WISE</em> data included the investigation of outlier populations, among them objects which were only well detected in W3 and W4, including WISE\u00a01814+3412. This approach proved highly successful (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f1\">1</a>), leading to a variety of follow-up programs now underway on sources that are much fainter in W1 (3.4\u2009\u03bcm) and W2 (4.6\u2009\u03bcm) than W3 (12\u2009\u03bcm) or W4 (22\u2009\u03bcm). The selection criteria for these \"W1W2-dropouts\" are W1 &gt;17.4\u2009(&lt; 34\u2009\u03bcJy), and either (1) W4 &lt;7.7\u2009(&gt; 6.9\u00a0mJy) and W2 \u2212 W4 &gt; 8.2 or (2) W3 &lt;10.6\u2009(&gt; 1.7\u00a0mJy) and W2 \u2212 W3 &gt; 5.3. W1W2-dropouts must also have at least seven individual <em>WISE</em> exposures available for measurement in W3 or W4, be more than 30\u00b0 from the Galactic center and 10\u00b0 from the Galactic plane, be free of artifacts flagged by the <em>WISE</em> pipeline, not be associated with known asteroids (including asteroids discovered by <em>WISE</em>; Mainzer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r52\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r52\">2011a</a>), and finally pass a series of visual inspections of both the individual exposures and coadded images for evidence of spurious sources. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f1\">1</a> illustrates the rarity of W1W2-dropouts in <em>WISE</em> color\u2013color space. The sources have a surface density <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0.03\u2009deg<sup>\u22122</sup>, or &lt;10<sup>\u22125</sup> that of <em>WISE</em> sources in general. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f1\">1</a> also shows the distribution of the 143 redshifts currently known for the sample. Over 60% of W1W2-dropouts are at <em>z</em> &gt; 1.6, with most of the low-redshift population optically bright, as noted by Bridge et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apj436934r10\" id=\"fnref-apj436934r10\">2012</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj436934f1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj436934f1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934f1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934f1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0<em>WISE</em> color\u2013color plot of the 226,017 sources with &lt;0.3\u00a0mag errors in W1 (3.4\u2009\u03bcm), W2 (4.6\u2009\u03bcm), and W3 (12\u2009\u03bcm), in the 313.4\u00a0deg<sup>2</sup> area of the all-sky release catalog with Galactic latitude <em>b</em> &gt; 80\u00b0 (black points and gray regions). These represent about 8% of all <em>WISE</em> catalog sources in this region. The larger red points are for 907 W1W2-dropouts selected over the whole sky, excluding the Galactic plane and bulge, an area ~100 times larger, illustrating the rarity of the W12drop population. The inset shows the histogram of available redshifts for the W12drop sample, with the cyan shading corresponding to W1W2-dropouts not seen in the optical digitized sky survey. The contours are in surface density at power-law steps of <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934ieqn2.gif\" alt=\"$\\sqrt{2}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sqrt{2}</script></span></span>, with the outermost contour at 10 sources per 0.05\u2009mag <b>&#x00d7;</b> 0.05\u00a0mag. The colors plotted for the W1W2-dropouts use <em>Spitzer</em> 3.6 and 4.5\u2009\u03bcm follow-up data since they are generally not detected at 3.4 and 4.6\u2009\u03bcm by <em>WISE</em>. <em>Spitzer</em> data were converted to the <em>WISE</em> system using [3.4] \u2212 [4.6] = <em>W</em>1 \u2212 <em>W</em>2 = 1.4([3.6] \u2212 [4.5]), and [4.6] \u2212 [12] = <em>W</em>2 \u2212 <em>W</em>3 = 0.07 + [4.5] \u2212 [12]. The large blue symbol with error bars is for WISE\u00a01814+3412. The vertical dashed line at <em>W</em>2 \u2212 <em>W</em>3 = 5.3 is one of the selection criteria for W1W2-dropouts; some are bluer than this because they satisfied the <em>W</em>2 \u2212 <em>W</em>4 &gt; 8.2 criterion.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj436934f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/downloadFigure/figure/apj436934f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj436934f1\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj436934f1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>WISE\u00a01814+3412 satisfies all these selection criteria, and was initially identified in 2010 May using similar criteria and available data. Of the 37 sources that satisfied the criteria at the time, WISE\u00a01814+3412 was the brightest in W4. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apj436934f2\">2</a> shows cutouts of images in the four <em>WISE</em> bands of WISE\u00a01814+3412, and Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apj436934t1\">1</a> gives W3 and W4 point-spread function fitted photometry for the source. In the two shorter bands the 2\u03c3 limit for W1 is formally &gt;18.67\u2009(&lt; 11\u2009\u03bcJy) although magnitudes at these levels appear to underestimate the true flux density, while there is a marginal detection at W2 = 17.17 \u00b1 0.49\u2009(23 \u00b1 11\u2009\u03bcJy).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apj436934f2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj436934f2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934f2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/755/2/173/Full/apj436934f2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Top row: images of WISE\u00a01814+3412 in the <em>WISE</em> W1 (3.4\u2009\u03bcm), W2 (4.6\u2009\u03bcm), W3 (12\u2009\u03bcm), and W4 (22\u2009\u03bcm) bands, from left to right. Second row: follow-up images at <em>r</em>' (0.63\u2009\u03bcm), 3.6\u2009\u03bcm, 350\u2009\u03bcm, and 4.5\u00a0GHz (6.8\u00a0cm), from left to right. Images are 2 arcmin on a side, with north up and east to the left. Circles are 10'' in radius, centered on the <em>WISE</em> source position. The counterpart to the southern radio source apparent in the 6.8\u00a0cm image is indicated with a blue arrow in the 0.63 and 3.6\u2009\u03bcm images.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apj436934f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/downloadFigure/figure/apj436934f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apj436934f2\" href=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/downloadHRFigure/figure/apj436934f2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apj436934t1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0Photometry for WISE\u00a01814+3412</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apj436934t1\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/755/2/173/suppdata/apj436934t1_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 1\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col><col align=\"left\"></col></colgroup><thead>\n<tr valign=\"top\"><th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Band</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u03bb</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Instrument</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Flux Density</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Comp.
